Master Thesis
The Eﬀect of Entrapped Air on Drying
Droplets in a Spray Dryer
Author:
Line Riis Madsen
Student at the Technical University of Denmark
Supervisors:
Ole Hassager
Professor at DTU, Danish Polymer Center
Christian Fridberg
Manager of Process R&D, SPX
January 24th 2016
Master thesis
Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The subject of this thesis is modeling of a single droplet drying when air is entrapped inside. The
model by Handscomb[1] was chosen as the base model on which further work was done, since the
model is based on a droplet containing both solvent, solute and possible insoluble or crystallized
particles. The models for the continuous and discrete phase was derived and implemented, however,
the implementation of the discrete phase proved troublesome, and thus was not completed. Fur-
thermore, the heat balance was not derived, but instead a simpliﬁed expression for the total mass
rate of evaporation was adopted.
The continuous phase was simulated using the ﬁnite diﬀerence method on the spatial variable, and
solving the remaining ordinary diﬀerential equation in time in MATLAB®. The implementation
was done in several steps with increasing complexity, with the ﬁnal one being simulation of the
continuous phase with a centrally located air bubble inside, where both the inner and outer radius
of the droplet were simple functions of time.
In the ﬁnal part of this thesis, balances describing the inner and outer radius of the droplet when
also taking into account the mass rate of evaporation and droplet temperature, was put forth. These
were used for simulation of two diﬀerent cases for the mass rate of evaporation; constant mass rate
of evaporation and decreasing mass rate of evaporation and thus increase in droplet temperature,
as would be expected when a shell begins to form. The two cases were combined and the ballooning
eﬀect, caused by expansion of the internal air bubble, was observed. However, the constants used
in the heat balance was assumed to be constant values throughout the entire drying, and thus
not functions of temperature or droplet composition, and may therefore give a faulty simulation.
However, the model put forth in this thesis is a starting point for a complete model describing
drying of droplets with entrapped air.

Preface
This report is the ﬁnal thesis that completes the master degree "Chemical and Bio-Chemical Engi-
neering" at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). This report is a result of the collaboration
between The Danish Polymer Center (DPC) at DTU, Department of Chemical and Biochemical
Engineering and SPX Flow Technology Denmark A/S. The literature study, model derivation and
expansion have been conducted from August 24th 2015 to January 24th 2016. The ﬁnal report was
handed in January 24th 2016.
I would like to thank my supervisors Professor Ole Hassager, Department of Chemical Engineering,
DPC, and Christian Fridberg, SPX Flow Technology Denmark A/S, whose grand knowledge and
competent guidance has helped me to understand, derive and expand the model.
Furthermore, I would like to give a special thanks to my partner, Steen Müller Christensen, who
has been kind to help with proofreading of the thesis but especially for the moral support in the
last 5 months.

Problem Statement
Project Description
Project title:
The Eﬀect of Entrapped Air on Drying Droplets in a Spray Dryer
Project type:
Master Thesis
Points:
30 ECTS
Student:
Line Riis Madsen, s103149 (Lineriismadsen@gmail.com)

August 24th 2015 to January 24th 2016
Supervisors:
Ole Hassager, Professor at DTU, Danish Polymer Center (oh@kt.dtu.dk)
Christian Fridberg, Manager of Process R&D, SPX Flow Technology
Denmark A/S (Christian.fridberg@spxﬂow.com)
Location:
The project will be conducted at DTU and SPX
Background
Spray drying is the technique used for drying a variety of diﬀerent products, ranging from skim milk
to high value pharmaceuticals, with the list continuously increasing. Even though the technique is
widely used and fairly simple, the details of drying droplets are not fully described in the literature
and thus attention to this area is important. By having an accurate model to describe the drying of
a droplet and the dried droplets ﬁnal morphology, the amount of time and money spend to develop
the drying environment for diﬀerent feeds would decrease signiﬁcantly.
Several models exists which describe the drying of droplets containing fully dissolved or insoluble
particles, and some models describe droplets containing both. However, models describing drying
of droplets containing entrapped air as well are limited, even though it is a well known phenomena
within spray drying.
Purpose
The purpose of this thesis is to derive a model for a drying droplet which from the beginning contains
entrapped air in the form of small air bubbles. The model will build on other already established
and well-known models.
Content
This thesis consists of four main parts:
• Literature study to expand knowledge on drying droplets, and to investigate already estab-
lished models.
• Derivation and implementation of an already established model.
• Expansion of established model to model drying of droplets with entrapped air as well.
If time permits, the newly formed model will be validated by comparison with experimental data,
and it will also be simulated in a computational ﬂuid dynamics software.

Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1 Motivation
Spray drying is a widely used technique and the use of it continues to increase. The reason why
spray drying is such an attractive drying technique is because it can be customized to the product
in hand. When spray drying pharmaceuticals the most important thing is to maintain a sterile
environment, whereas when drying food the prime interest is to keep nutrition and aromas in the
dried product. Furthermore, it is important that the dried product has the physical properties that
the customer demands and recognizes, e.g. density, porosity, and solubility. In order to obtain all
of the above requirements, the spray dryer needs to be tailored for each speciﬁc product.
The essential part of a spray dryer is atomization of the feed and subsequently contacting the
spray with hot drying air. In order to obtain the desired chemical and physical properties of the
dried particles, the feed composition and the drying environment needs to be speciﬁed accordingly.
Besides atomization and subsequently drying, the drying process may be aﬀected by several other
factors, e.g. agglomeration of particles on the dryer wall and agglomeration of particles themselves.
For those working with spray drying, a comprehensive model of spray drying taking into account the
droplet drying, heat ﬂux, agglomeration and the physical environment, is desired since this would
make it easier to specify the drying environment for each individual product. Despite extensive
research and several attempts such a model does not exist today.
Another approach for modeling spray dryers is to model each part of the technique by individual
models, e.g. a theoretical model for drying a single droplet, using computational ﬂuid dynamic
(CFD) for modeling the ﬂux of drying medium, and subsequently coupling these together. Detailed
models of single drying droplets do not only model the moisture content of the droplet, but also
the progress of the physical description of the droplet. The droplet in these types of models may
consist of dissolved or insoluble particles, however, the presence of entrapped air is usually neglected.
Entrapped air bubbles emerge during pumping and atomization of the feed. The aim of this thesis
is to develop and validate a model of a single drying droplet with entrapped air bubbles inside.
1.2 Structure of Thesis
This chapter is an introduction to the motivation behind the chosen subject. Following this, Chapter
2 will present background information on the spray drying technique and equipment, as well as
description of the mechanism of a single drying droplet.
Chapter 3 will present the four most
common methods for setting up a drying model. In the last part of the chapter a discussion about
the model by C.S. Handscomb[1] will be given, since this is the chosen model upon which future
work will be conducted.
The model by Handscomb is divided into two parts; before and after shell formation, which may be
sub categorized further into models for continuous and discrete phase, and the energy model. In
Chapter 4 the model by Handscomb will be derived for the regime prior to shell formation.
1
==PAGE:16==
1.2 Structure of Thesis
Chapter 5 introduces the ﬁnite diﬀerence solution method, which is the technique used to solve the
models put forth in this thesis. Furthermore, several simpliﬁed models of the continuous and the
discrete phase will be implemented and solved in MATLAB®. In Chapter 5 the simpliﬁed models
for the continuous phase are solved when assuming that the droplet radius is constant, while in
Chapter 6 the simpliﬁed models are solved when the radius is a function of time, and a centrally
located air bubble is present. The functions used for the inner and outer radius are simple linear
expressions, but in Chapter 7 a more complex set of equations for the inner and outer radius of the
droplet is implemented. Furthermore, in Chapter 7 the complete model is used to simulate drying
of a droplet with a centrally located air bubble.
A discussion of the obtained model and the assumptions made in order to solve it, will be given in
Chapter 8. Furthermore, the future work that needs to be done on the model will also be discussed.
The ﬁnal chapter, Chapter 9 is a conclusion of the complete thesis.
Furthermore it should be mentioned that citations are given as [1] while references to footnotes
are given as 1 and references to equations are given as (1.1), where the numbers are those of the
reference, footnote or equation, respectively.

==PAGE:17==
Chapter 2
Spray Drying
Spray drying is a widely used technique for drying of a large variety of products. The application
ranges from common commodities such as detergents or milk powder, over high value pharmaceu-
ticals, to electrical insulation materials. The spray drying unit may be the key unit operation, such
as in the production of soup of coﬀee powder, or it may be an intermediate unit operation, such as
in the the production of leather shoes, where the tanin used for curing of the leather is produced
by spray drying. Consequently, spray drying is a widely used unit operation whose application
continues to increase[2,3].
Spray drying is an attractive drying operation since it converts a liquid feed into solid particles, and
it is possible to produce end-materials with precise quality standards. The primary particle size
of the dry particles are 10-500 µm, with a residence time in the chamber of 5-100 s[2]. The only
requirement for the feed is that it should be pumpable, thus both solutions, suspensions, emulsions
and pastes may pass as feeds for the spray dryer. A spray-dryer is easy to operate, and powder
characteristics remain constant throughout a continuous operation when the drying conditions are
held constant. It is possible to produce a variety of dryer designs with a large range of drying
capacities, such that the equipment can be designed for diﬀerent requirements, which is also the
reason for the large diversity of application of this unit operation. However, the disadvantages of
the process is that it has a low thermal eﬃciency, has a high installation cost, and the equipment
is large[2]. The general setup of a spray dryer will be discussed in the following section.
2.1 Setup
Spray drying involves four process stages; atomization of liquid feed into a spray, inﬂow of drying
medium in a speciﬁc spray-air ﬂow, evaporation of the spray, and lastly separation of the dried
particles from the drying medium. The process stages are summarized in Figure 2.1. The speciﬁc
elements of each process stage will be discussed further below, but ﬁrst a general description of the
most common types of spray drying will be presented.
Separation 
Product discharge 
from chamber and 
separation unit 
Total product 
discharge from 
separation unit 
Atomization 
Centrifugal

nozzle

Spray-Air Contact

current 
Mixed 
Spray Evaporation

Powder 
Figure 2.1: Schematic representation of process stages within spray drying. Adapted from
Handscomb [1].
3
==PAGE:18==
2.1 Setup
The vast majority of spray dryers evaporate moisture from droplets by being sprayed into a hot
atmosphere, which is also the type used throughout this thesis. However, several other types of
spray dryers exists, and they will be summarized shortly. Some products are very delicate and
thus the drying medium is dehumidiﬁed and merely warmed over atmospheric temperatures before
entering the spray dryer. This type of spray drying is termed low-temperature spray drying. Another
spray drying technique for maintaining low drying temperatures are denoted pulse combustion spray
drying, since it utilizes heat and acoustics. If a very low bulk density of the dried product is desired
foam spray drying could be employed, since air is introduced to the feed before atomization which
forms a foam like particle with air vacuoles inside. Instead of using heat to evaporate the spray
one could also spray the feed into freezing air, which would cause the droplets to freeze and the
moisture is subsequently removed by sublimation in vacuum. This type of spray drying is termed
spray freeze drying or spray cooling if the low temperature only permits the droplets to solidify.
Instead of using the spray dryer solely to dry a feed, one could also use it to perform chemical
reactions inside. Spray reaction is when the sprayed particles reacts with the hot gas medium to
produce a new chemical compound. The reaction rate can be fairly high since the surface area of
the sprayed droplets is large. A variation of spray reaction is spray dryer adsorption, where a toxic
gas is adsorbed by the spray.
2.1.1
Atomization
The ﬁrst process within spray drying is atomization of the feed. It is a crucial step since this is
where the spray is formed and the nature of the droplets are established.
There are three main types of atomizers; centrifugal atomizer, pressure nozzle, and two-ﬂuid nozzle.
The spray pattern of these are shown in Figure 2.2. With centrifugal atomizers the feed is introduced
centrally onto a rotating wheel or disc where the centrifugal energy causes the feed to atomize when
it leaves the plate. Wheel centrifugal atomizers produces ﬁne to medium-coarse droplets, while
disc centrifugal atomizers produce coarse droplets, with the general mean size of droplets formed
by centrifugal atomizer at 20-150 µm. When nozzles are used as atomizers, the feed is introduced
to the drying chamber through a small nozzle, which due to pressure or kinetic energy transforms
the liquid feed to a spray. The mean size of droplets formed with nozzle atomizers are 120-300
µm[2]. With a two-ﬂuid nozzle the atomization is pneumatic, since a compressed gas, usually air,
is brought in contact with the feed, which causes atomization to occur[3]. The rule of thumb is
that a single centrifugal atomizer is able to handle larger feeds than a single nozzle atomizer[2],
(a) Centrifugal atomizer. Reprinted from [4].
(b) Nozzle atomizer.
Reprinted from [5].
Figure 2.2: Spray patterns from atomizers used in spray dryers.
4
==PAGE:19==
2.1 Setup
however, usually multiple nozzles can be placed in a dryer if the feedrate is large. Other advantages
of centrifugal atomizers are that they are easy to operate, operates at low pressure, can handle
ﬂuctuating feedrates, and that the droplet size is easily adjusted by adjusting the rotary velocity.
The advantages of nozzle atomizers is that the design of these varies signiﬁcantly, making it possible
to specify the design for a given process.
2.1.2
Spray-air Contact
The ﬂow of the drying medium, which is usually air, can be co-current, counter-current or mixture
of these.
In a co-current spray dryer the drying medium and the feed enters the drying chamber at the same
end. Thus, the hottest drying medium (150-220℃) meets the wet droplets, and the dried particles
are in the drying medium with the lowest temperature (50-80℃)[6]. This process is often used when
the product is heat sensitive, since the product is not subjected to heat degradation[2]. Counter-
current ﬂow is when the drying medium and the feed is introduced into the chamber in opposite
directions. This type of ﬂow causes excellent heat utilization, however, the dry product is subjected
to the hottest air, making it impossible to use this air ﬂow for heat sensitive products. Furthermore,
it is only possible to have counter-current air-spray pattern when one uses nozzles as the atomizer.
2.1.3
Spray Evaporation
As soon as the droplets are dispersed into the hot drying medium, evaporation of the solvent begins.
The general drying mechanism of a single droplet is given in Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Schematic representation of a drying droplet. Reprinted from Huang [7].
At point 0 in Figure 2.3, the droplet enters the hot chamber and evaporation begins. The temper-
ature of the droplet increases to the wet-bulb temperature, point 1, and stays at this level for a
given amount of time. The period from point 1 to 2 is termed the constant rate period, since the
rate of evaporation of solvent from the surface of the droplet is constant. As solvent evaporates the
size of the droplet decreases. At point 2 a shell is formed, and from this point on the size of the
droplet do not change signiﬁcantly, but the temperature of the droplet increases until it reaches the
temperature of the surrounding environment at point 3. The rate of evaporation from point 2 to 3
is decreasing, due to the increasing diﬀusion limitation imposed by the shell[1,7,8].
The morphology of the ﬁnal dried particles varies signiﬁcantly, with drying environment, droplet
characteristics and spray-drying equipment. The crucial step for determining the ﬁnal morphology of
5
==PAGE:20==
2.1 Setup
Figure 2.4: Simpliﬁes schematic showing some of the diﬀerent kinds
of morphologies that can be obtained when drying droplets. Reprinted
from Handscomb [1].
Figure 2.5:
Setup for separation of
dried powder from a co-current spray
dryer. Reprinted from Handscomb [1].
the dried droplet is in the falling-rate period, hence after the shell has formed. With the shell present
two major drying mechanisms are believed to be possible; wet-shell and dry-shell mechanisms[1,2,9].
In the wet-shell mechanism it is believed that the shell will be wetted at all times, whereas in the
dry-shell mechanism the shell is dry and moisture thus have to diﬀusive out through the shell before
evaporating from the droplet. Figure 2.4 displays some of the morphologies that can be obtained
when drying droplets, and from here it is seen that a fully solid particle can be obtained with the
dry shell mechanism, whereas a particle with an air bubble of some kind inside is usually obtained
when the droplet follows the wet shell mechanism.
2.1.4
Separation
The last step of spray drying is to separate the dry particles from the drying medium. This is
usually done in a manner such that the majority of the dried powder leaves the spray dryer from
the bottom of the chamber, while air and remaining powder exist the dryer through a separation
unit, e.g. cyclone or bag ﬁlter. This setup is shown in Figure 2.5.
6
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 3
Models of Drying Droplets
In this chapter a short recap of the main methods used for models of single drying droplets will be
given.
3.1 Method
The methods used in the models for drying droplets as presented in the literature, can be summarized
by the following[8]:
• Characteristic drying curve (CDC)
• Reaction engineering approach (REA)
• Conservation equations
• Population balance equation (PBE)
In the following each methods will be presented along side the advantages and disadvantages of it.
3.1.1
Characteristic Drying Curve
The CDC method is semi-empirical. It utilizes data from a series of experiments to ﬁnd an expression
for the drying rate. It assumes that the drying rate is constant up to the point of critical moisture
content, hence the drying will be of the following form[10,11]:
˙N = ˙Ncr f(φ)
(3.1.1)
where ˙N is the drying rate, ˙Ncr is drying rate up until the critical moisture content and φ is the
dimensionless moisture content, given as:
φ = X −Xe
Xcr −Xe
(3.1.2)
where X is the instantaneous moisture content, Xe and Xcr are the equilibrium and critical moisture
content, respectively.
CDC models are simple and consists only of a small set of equations, which allows for fast calcu-
lations, and the method seems to work well with small droplets[8]. However, it assumes that the
critical moisture content is determined solely from the material, which is not true since it is aﬀected
by the drying environment[10,11]. Additionally, it is assumed that the shape of the drying curve is
not a function of the drying environment, e.g. the drying temperature, which is a poor assumption
since the viscosity and surface tension of the droplet will be aﬀected by this, which in turn will
aﬀect the drying rate. From this it can be concluded that the model works well for small droplets,
and if the drying environment do not change signiﬁcantly from that used to obtain the model[8].
3.1.2
Reaction Engineering Approach
The REA assumes that evaporation is an activation process that has to overcome an energy bar-
rier[12], and additionally that the activation energy is a function of the moisture content in the
7
==PAGE:22==
3.1 Method
droplet which is a characteristic property of each material. Assuming so, the model for the drying
rate is given by[11,12]:
dm
dt = −hm A (ρv,s −ρv,b)
(3.1.3)
m is the mass of the droplet, hm the mass transfer coeﬃcient, A the surface area of the droplet, and
ρv,s and ρv,b is the vapor concentration at the gas-droplet interface and in the bulk, respectively.
ρv,s is an unknown time-dependent parameter, that can be estimated from the following equation:
ρv,s = ψ ρv,sat(Ts)
(3.1.4)
ψ is an expression for the surface relative humidity and ρv,sat is the saturated vapor concentration
at the particle-gas interface where the surface temperature is Ts. In the REA the express for ψ is
assumed to be given by the following simple expression[11]:
ψ = exp
 
−∆Ev
Rgas Ts
!
(3.1.5)
in which ∆Ev is the additional activation energy for drying caused by the increasing diﬃculty in
removing water at a low moisture content within the droplet. Furthermore, if the temperature
range in the dryer is not too wide, hence less than 100 K, the expression for the saturated vapor
concentration may be given by the following Arrhenius relation[12]:
ρv,sat = Kv exp

−Ev
R Ts

(3.1.6)
Here Kv is the apparent reaction frequency and Ev is the activation energy for evaporation of the
solvent[11].
Using the REA gives a model that is easy to compute, and the result is also in ﬁne agreement with
experimental data. Before using the method, one needs to experimentally determine the value of
the activation energy and the apparent reaction frequency, such that ψ and ρv,sat can be found.
Thus, the REA model is limited in range to those materials where these characteristics are already
known[8].
3.1.3
Conservation Equation
When setting up deterministic drying models using conservation equations, the equations of interest
is continuity, momentum, energy and species conservation[8]. The general formula of these are given
below[1,13,14].
The equation of continuity:
∂ρ
∂t + ∇· (ρv) = 0
(3.1.7)
The equation of continuity for species i:
∂ρi
∂t + ∇· (ρivi) = ri
(3.1.8)
The equation of motion:
ρDv
Dt = −∇· Π + ρg
(3.1.9)
8
==PAGE:23==
3.1 Method
The equation of energy:
ρ Dh
Dt = −∇· q + Dp
Dt + ∇v : τ + Φ
(3.1.10)
The meaning of the symbols are as follows; ρ is the mass density, v the velocity vector, and ri is
the mass rate of production of species i by homogeneous chemical reaction. Π is the total stress
tensor, g the gravitational acceleration vector, h the enthalpy per mass, q the heat ﬂux vector, p
the pressure, τ the viscous stress tensor, and Φ is the rate of heat generation. Furthermore, it has
been used that:
DB
Dt = ∂B
∂t + v · ∇B
(3.1.11)
where B may be a scalar or a vector.
In order to ﬁnd the drying mechanism of the droplet the above set of equations have to be solved,
with a set of appropriate initial (IC) and boundary conditions (BC). This is a cumbersome process
because of the moving domain boundary, which in the constant rate drying period is the radius of
the droplet, and in the falling rate period it is the receding interface between shell and core of the
droplet. This requires signiﬁcant computer resources and time. Furthermore, many material speciﬁc
coeﬃcients have to be known at varying moisture and temperature conditions, e.g. particle porosity,
critical moisture content, etc.. Most likely these can not be found in the published literature, and
thus experiments have to be conducted in order to determined these. However, the obtained results
of the drying mechanism of the droplet is usually in close agreement with experimental data, and
the model may be applied at varying drying conditions[8].
3.1.4
Population Balance Equation
PBE are used for modeling of solids components within a droplet, thus PBE are coupled with
conservation equations for the continuous phase of the droplet, e.g. the liquid phase. When using
PBE for a drying droplet, an internal and external coordinate system is usually set up, where the
internal describes the size and shape of the solid particle, and the external describes the location of
the particle in the droplet. The general form of the PBE is given as[1]:
∂N
∂t + ∇x · XN + ∇r · RN = ∇x ·

Dx∇xDT
x

+ ∇r ·

Dr∇rDrT
+ ˙h
(3.1.12)
Here N is a number density function, X the state vector of the internal coordinates, R the state
vector of the external coordinates, and subscripts of x and r refers to the internal and external
coordinate system, respectively. D is a vector with partial diﬀerential equation diﬀusive terms, and
˙h is the source term of the population balance, hence birth and death term of the particles. The
second and third term on left hand side of the equation describes the advection of particles, and
the ﬁrst and second term on the right hand side of Equation (3.1.12) describes diﬀusion of the solid
particles.
As with the conservation equations, the PBE is a very cumbersome model that requires signiﬁcant
computational power and time. However, the PBE is able to model the solid phase of the droplet
accurately, and thus is essential for prediction of the morphology of the dried droplet and other
structural quantities of interest, e.g. the average size of the solid particles that form the initial
surface shell[1].
9
==PAGE:24==
3.2 Model for Future Work
3.2 Model for Future Work
Instead of categorizing models according to the method use to derive them, one could also divide
them into categories according to the type of droplet they model. Usually models either describe
a droplet containing insoluble solids (slurry or suspension), droplets consisting solely of dissolved
solids (solutions), or droplets that contain both insoluble and dissolved solids.
In a review by
Mezhericher et al.[8] several models of each category were presented and discussed, including the
model by Handscomb et al.[1,9,15].
The model derived by Handscomb et al. is a theoretical model of droplets containing a continuous
phase of an ideal binary mixture and a discrete phase consisting of insoluble solid particles. The
model was derived by using conservation equations for the continuous phase and PBE for the discrete
phase. In both drying stages a uniform temperature in the droplet was assumed due to a low Biot
number. Contrary to most published models, the model for Handscomb et al. did not assume
that the droplet size ceased to decrease as soon as a crust was formed, instead a shell-thickening
regime was believed to be present from the time of initial shell formation and until a rigid shell was
formed[1,8,9]. During this regime the size of the droplet continues to decrease. After formation of
the rigid crust Handscomb et al. operates with two types of drying models; the wet and the dry
shell models. With the wet shell model, the shell is wetted at all times and a central bubble is
formed, whereas in the dry shell model the shell is dry and moisture has to diﬀusive from the center
of the bubble to the surface of the droplet in order to evaporate.
The model by Handscomb et al.[1,9,15] is a cumbersome model, which requires signiﬁcant compu-
tational time, however, the results are in close agreement with experimental data and it is able to
predict the ﬁnal morphology of a complex drying droplet accurately[1,8,9,15,16]. For that reason this
model has been chosen as the basis model for which drying of droplets containing entrapped air will
be implemented to. In the next chapter the model will be described in detail and derived.
10
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 4
Handscomb Model Prior to Shell Formation
The model derived by Handscomb et al.[1,9,15] will be presented in this chapter. Due to limited
time it is only the ﬁrst part of the model, hence before shell formation, that will be derived in this
hand-in, however, the plan is to derive the second part as well.
4.1 Background
A droplet may contain solids dissolved in a solvent, insoluble solids, and entrapped air. A schematic
representation of such a droplet is given in Figure 4.1. However, the model developed by Handscomb
et al. do not include entrapped air bubbles. Moreover, the model by Handscomb et al. assumes
that the liquid phase is an ideal binary mixture, from now on referred to as the continuous phase,
and that the solid phase consists of insoluble particles, from here on denoted the discrete phase.
Figure 4.1: Schematic representation of droplet containing in-
soluble solid particles and gas bubbles. Reprinted from Hand-
scomb [1].
The method used by Handscomb et al. for setting up a drying model is to use the conservation
equations (Section 3.1.3). Each phase must obey the equation of continuity, (3.1.7), the equation
of continuity for species, (3.1.8), and the equation of motion, (3.1.9). These equations are suﬃcient
to model the mass transfer within a droplet if the conﬁguration of the phase interfaces are known.
However, usually these are not known and even if they were, the computational time needed to
solve the model with the help of the known conﬁguration would be impractical and troublesome.
Instead local volume averages at each point within the structure is determined. The equations
of conservations are point equations meaning that they should be valid at each point, thus when
employing the volume averaging theorem these fundamental equations are averaged over a suitably
chosen region of the droplet[1,17,18]. This theorem is explained in the following subsection.
11
==PAGE:26==
4.1 Background
Figure 4.2: Illustration of an averaging surface S with volume V at the point
z. The fraction of the volume within the droplet, that is occupied by the contin-
uous phase, V (c), is bounded by the walls of the discrete phase and the overall
volume boundary. The volume of the discrete phase within the droplet is V (d).
The interfaces between discrete and continuous phase is denoted Sw and the in-
terfaces between the total averaging volume and the continuous phase is denoted
Se. Reprinted from Handscomb [1].
4.1.1
Local Volume Averaging
In this subsection the local volume averaging theorems will be presented. For a thorough derivation
of the presented equations the reader is referred to Appendix A.
Every point z within a porous droplet, regardless of the phase the point is placed in, has an averaging
surface S associated with it. In Figure 4.2 the averaging surface is drawn as a circle, however, it
may take on any shape as long as it is invariant. The total volume within S is V where V (α) is the
volume occupied by phase α. In this derivation it is assumed that the droplet only consists of 2
phases, the continuous and the discrete phase.
When considering a quantity B, which may either be a scalar, vector or a tensor, the average of
this within S may be deﬁned in following three ways.
Total volume average:
⟨B⟩≡1

(4.1.1)

the intrinsic volume average is the average of B within V (α), and the superﬁcial volume average

4.1.3
Droplet Temperature
The temperature gradient within the droplet have been ignored throughout the model, due the
assumption of Biot number less than 0.1[1]. The Biot may be deﬁned as follows:
Bi = hE L0
k
(4.1.18)
where hE is the heat transfer coeﬃcient of the mass transfer ﬁlm, L0 is a characteristic length, and
k is the thermal conductivity of the droplet. The Biot number can be explained as the ratio of the
internal heat transfer resistance to the external heat transfer resistance, thus at a low Biot number,
the largest resistance to heat transfer is outside the droplet and uniform droplet temperature may
be assumed.
Some systems may not be covered by this assumption, and thus temperature gradients within the
droplet has to be considered. However, the model by Handscomb et al. does not include this.
4.1.4
Notation
Throughout this model quantities relating to the solvent, solute and solid components have been
given the subscripts A, B and D respectively.
Furthermore, when looking at quantities in the
continuous or discrete phase they have been given the superscript (c) or (d), and the porosity of
the droplet, i.e. the volume fraction of the discrete phase, is denoted by ε.
The term droplet refers to the overall droplet being dried, particle refers to the solid particles within
the drying droplet i.e. the discrete phase, and dried-particle is the droplet at the ﬁnal end of drying.

4.2 Continuous Phase
It is assumed that the continuous phase consists of a ideal binary mixture, thus when the mass
fraction of one of the components is known, the complete phase is speciﬁed. Consequently, it has
been decided only to solve the mass balance for the solute, since then the crystallization process

4.3 Discrete Phase
The discrete phase is modeled by a population balance, which describes the time evolution of
a particle density function, N. The particle density function is a function of solid particle size,
placement of the particle within the droplet, and time. The particles and the droplet considered in
this model are assumed to be spherical, and thus the placement of the particles within the droplet
may be described by a single internal and external coordinate. The particle diameter, L, is chosen
as the internal coordinate, and thus the state space of the particle is [Lmin, ∞], where Lmin is the
minimum stable crystal size, which must be determined experimentally. The external coordinate to
describe the placement of the particle within the spherical droplet is the radius, r. The state space
of r is [b + Lmin
2 , R −Lmin
2 ], where b is the radius of the central bubble, and R is the radius of the
entire droplet.
Figure 4.3 illustrates the particle density function, N, as a function of r and L. As can be seen
from the ﬁgure, the size of the solid particles are smallest close to the center of the droplet, while
increasing with increasing droplet radius.
The general population balance was introduced in Section 3.1.4, and the balance adapted to this
speciﬁc situation is as follows:
∂N
∂t + ∂

where spherical symmetry has been assumed. G is the growth rate of the particles, which is assumed
to be linear and independent of particle size and the continuous phase ﬂow, i.e G ̸= f(L, ⟨vr⟩(c)).
These assumptions are valid if the solid particles are smaller than 50 µm[1].
It is furthermore
assumed that the discrete phase only grows due to particle growth or nucleation of new particle
with the size Lmin but not due to agglomeration or breakage of particles.
The overall discrete phase may move in the continuous phase due to convection or diﬀusion or a
combination of the two, where D(d) is the diﬀusion coeﬃcient of the solid phase in the continuous
phase and v(d)
r
is the velocity of the particles. It is assumed that before shell formation, the particles
only move due to diﬀusion, i.e. v(d)
r
= 0, and furthermore after shell formation the solid particles
do not move at all[1]. Thus particle growth is the only mechanism operating after shell formation.
4.3.1
Boundary Conditions
At all times new particles may form trough nucleation. The rate of nucleation of new particles per
unit volume per unit time, ˙N0, is given as the growth rate times the particle density function in the
low region of the internal coordinates:
˙N0 = G N(Lmin, r, t)
(4.3.2)
This nucleation rate may also be seen as the ﬂux of particles across the low boundary of the particle
size. At the upper boundary of the internal coordinate, L →∞, the number density function must
approach zero, N →0.
It is assumed that no solid particles leave the droplet, thus before shell formation the receding droplet
surface must cause the particles to be transport towards the center of the droplet. Consequently,
the ﬂux within the droplet must be equal to the ﬂux caused by the shrinking droplet, i.e.:
−D(d) ∂N

It is not necessary to know how the number density function is at all times, but rather knowing

Several of the lower integer moments, which are listed in Table 4.1, are of interest. The zeroth
moment is the total number of particles per unit volume, the ﬁrst moment is the total length or
diameter of the particles per unit volume, the second moment is the surface area of particles per
unit volume, and the third moment is the volume of particles per unit volume.

4.3.3
Diﬀusion Constant
Instead of a constant diﬀusion constant for the solid particles in the continuous phase, it is often
desired to have a size dependent diﬀusion constant. For this purpose Stokes-Einstein equation[13]
has been used:
D(d) = kbT
3πµL

4.4 Heat Balance
In order to obtain a full model for the drying droplet, a heat balance have to be set up as well
in order to obtain an expression the total mass rate of evaporation of A, mvap. However, due to
limited time it has been decided not to derive the exact expression for mvap, but instead assume
that mvap has the arbitrarily chosen constant value of:

==PAGE:43==
Chapter 5
Implementation of Simple Model With Constant
Boundary
At this point the models for the continuous and the discrete phase of the droplet have been derived,
and thus it is desired to implement a simpliﬁed version of these. This will be done in several steps
with decreasing simplicity.
Handscomb et al.[1,9,15] used the ﬁnite volume solution method, FV, to solve the model. However,
the author of this thesis has previous experience with the ﬁnite diﬀerence solution method, FD, but
non with FV and thus it has been decided to use FD to solve the model. Furthermore, Handscomb
et al. used fortran to solve the model, but the implementation program of choice during this
thesis is MATLAB®, also due to previous experience.
If nothing else is stated the values used for simulations are the those listed in Table 5.1.
Table 5.1: Values used for simulation
Constant
Symbol
Value
Droplet radius
R
1 · 10−3 m
Spatial nodes
n
100
Simulated time
-
100 s
Time nodes
-
100
The droplet radius of 1 mm and the simulated drying time of 100 s have been chosen since these are
within normal values with spray drying, as stated in Chapter 2. The amount of spatial and time
nodes will be discussed in Chapter 8.
5.1 Finite Diﬀerence Solution Method
When using FD to solve a diﬀerential model, the solution domain is divided into n nodes where
the discretization starts at x1 and ends at xn+1, hence xi = (i −1)∆x, as shown in Figure 5.1.
One could also start the notation at x0 and end at xn, but since the obtained models are to be
implemented in MATLAB® where one can not have a 0th element in a vector, it has been decided
to start the notation at i = 1 in order to directly implement the FD.

5.1 Finite Diﬀerence Solution Method
The derivatives of f(x) are approximated as ﬁnite diﬀerences, using a forward Taylor series expansion
of the function of interest:
f(xi + ∆x) = f(xi) + f′(xi)∆x + f′′(xi)
2!
∆x2 + f(3)(xi)
3!
∆x3 + O(∆x4)
⇔
(5.1.1)
f(xi+1) = f(xi) + f′(xi)∆x + f′′(xi)
2!
∆x2 + f(3)(xi)
3!
∆x3 + O(∆x4)
(5.1.2)
Rearranging yields the following expression for f′(xi):
f′(xi) = f(xi+1) −f(xi)
∆x
−f′′(xi)
2!
∆x + O(∆x2)
(5.1.3)
= f(xi+1) −f(xi)
∆x
+ O(∆x)
(5.1.4)
If instead of ﬁnding the Taylor series for f(xi + ∆x) one ﬁnds it for f(xi −∆x), a backward Taylor
series expansion, the following relation can be found:
f(xi −∆x) = f(xi) −f′(xi)∆x + f′′(xi)
2!
∆x2 −f(3)(xi)
3!
∆x3 + O(∆x4)
⇔
(5.1.5)
f′(xi) = f(xi) −f(xi−1)
∆x
+ O(∆x)
(5.1.6)
Thus, using either a forward or a backwards ﬁnite diﬀerence approximation results in a truncation
error of O(∆x), meaning that if the step size, ∆x, is halved, the truncation error will be approxi-
mately halved as well. An alternative to the forward or the backward approximation is the centered

5.2 Simpliﬁed Model for Continuous Phase
ﬁnite diﬀerence approximation of a ﬁrst order derivative. If (5.1.5) is subtracted from (5.1.2), the
following expressions emerge:
f(xi+1) −f(xi−1) = 2f′(xi)∆x + 2 f(3)(xi)
3!
∆x3 + O(∆x5)
(5.1.7)
By rearranging, the following expression for f′(xi) is obtained:
f′(xi) = f(xi+1) −f(xi−1)
2∆x
−f(3)(xi)
3!
∆x2 −O(∆x4)
(5.1.8)
= f(xi+1) −f(xi−1)
2∆x
−O(∆x2)
(5.1.9)
Thus, from the above expression it is seen that the truncation error made when using a centered
ﬁnite diﬀerence approximation of the ﬁrst derivative is of the order of ∆x2, hence, if the step size is
halved the truncation error would be quartered[19]. Consequently, the centered FD approximation
is more accurate. In Figure 5.2 a graphical representation of the three approximation types are
given Furthermore, the centered FD utilizes the step before and after that of interest, and since the
system to be modeled involves diﬀusion that may occur in either direction, the central FD is the
FD method of choice for this system.
If instead of subtracting (5.1.5) from (5.1.2) they are added together, one can obtain an approxi-
mation for the second derivative of f(xi):
f(xi+1) + f(xi−1) = 2f(xi) + f′′(xi)∆x2 + O(∆x4)
(5.1.10)
and by rearranging, the following expression for a centered FD approximation for f′′(xi) is derived:
f′′(xi) = f(xi+1) −2f(xi) + f(xi−1)
∆x2
−O(∆x2)
(5.1.11)
As can be seen, the truncation error for the second derivative is also of the order of ∆x2. For the
forward and backward FD approximations of the second derivative the truncation error is O(∆x)[19].
It should be noted that from here on, the notation is as follows:
f(xi) = fi
(5.1.12)
meaning that f has been discretized in a spatial direction, which in the above is x, but it may still
be a continuous function of time, hence f(xi, t) = fi(t).

fraction in the center decreases, which is consistent with the fact that an outwards radial ﬂux is
present. As can be seen from (b) steady state is achieved after approximately 60 s, meaning that the
diﬀusive and convective ﬂux are equal in size but with opposite directions from this point onwards.
In order to verify the simulated results, it is checked whether or not the boundary conditions are
satisﬁed, hence if the radiant mass ﬂux is zero in the center of the droplet and at the surface of it.
The radiant mass ﬂux is plotted as a function of radius in Figure 5.7 (b). It is seen that the radiant
ﬂux is largest near 0.6 mm, but 0 at the boundaries, thus the BC’s are satisﬁed. Furthermore, as
time passes steady state is achieved and the combined ﬂux becomes 0 everywhere.
Since the BC’s restrict matter from leaving the domain, the overall mass fraction of B should be
the same at all times, hence there must be mass conservation of B as described in (5.2.34). The
resulting ﬁgure of the total mass of B at each time step is given in Figure 5.7 (b), and it can be
concluded that conservation of mass is achieved, since the total mass of B is constant in time.

Figure 5.8 displays the mass fraction of B as a function of radius and time. Initially the mass fraction

conserved, which is also the conclusion from Figure 5.9 (b), where the total mass of B is plotted
against time. The total mass of B is calculated from (5.2.34). It should be noted that in the ﬁrst
10 seconds a small increase in mass is observed, but the increase is only 0.83 % and is therefore
ascribed to numerical errors when solving the model in MATLAB®.

A step in solving this simpliﬁed case was to use the non-constant but time and radius dependent
expression for the droplet density, ⟨ρ⟩(c), as determined in (4.2.29). When utilizing this expression
for the the density, it is possible to achieve a density gradient within the droplet, caused by an
uneven concentration of A and B within the droplet. Figure 5.10 (a) displays this phenomena by
plotting the density as a function of radius. Initially the mass fractions of B are non-uniform, and
thus a density gradient is also present, but as time passes the mass fraction of B and the density
become uniform within the droplet. Even though, a density gradient is present initially, the overall
droplet density is constant in time, as seen in Figure 5.10 (b). The overall droplet density has been
calculated by using the composite trapezoidal rule as presented in (5.2.36).

5.3 Simpliﬁed Model for Discrete Phase
Now the model for the continuous phase has been implemented in a simpliﬁed case, and the next
step is therefore to implement the model for the discrete phase in a simpliﬁed case as well. Two
simplifying cases will be implemented where the ﬁrst case assumes constant diﬀusion constant, while
the second case assumes that this is size-dependent. Furthermore, the following assumptions are
valid for both simplifying cases:
• The discrete phase only moves due to diﬀusion, hence v(d)
r
= 0.
• The size of the droplet is constant, and thus ˙m′′ = −dR
dt ρ0
A = 0.

Subﬁgure (a) depicts the zeroth moment, hence the number of particles per unit volume, (b) shows

as a function of time. From Figure 5.11 (a) it is seen that the initial non-uniform distribution
evens out as function of time, due to diﬀusive ﬂux, as would be expected. Furthermore it is found
from (b) that the average size of the particle diameter at all times and at all spatial positions is
Lmin = 0.05 mm, hence the particles do not grow. Figure (c) conﬁrms that no change of the particles
occurs, except of rearrangement, since the overall porosity of the droplet is constant in time.

Not surprisingly, the amount of particles, the size of these, and the overall porosity of the droplet

and blue line are exactly equal, as expected. From (b) it is seen that the average diameter of the

occurs. This is due to the formation of new particles with size Lmin at all times, which the growth
rate is to low too counterbalance. Figure 5.14 (c) shows that, as expected, the porosity of the
droplet is the largest when both new particles forms and when the existing ones grow.

The reason for this decrease in amount of particles is unknown, and may either result from a
wrongful model, incorrect assumptions, or a faulty implementation in matlab. It has been decided
not to put any more focus or time into solving this odd behavior within this thesis, but instead put
more energy into solving a more complex model for the continuous phase. Thus, yielding a ﬂawless
simulation of the discrete phase is a point of future work.

Chapter 6
Implementation of Simple Model With Moving
Boundary
A simpliﬁed version of the model for both the continuous and the discrete phase have been im-
plemented, in which it has been assumed that the droplet radius is constant. Therefore, the next
logical step is to simulate the simpliﬁed models when taking into account the movement of the outer
boundary, hence that the radius of the droplet is a function of time.
Initially the idea was to introduce air to the droplet in the form of small bubbles described by
the population balance, in a form similar to the one presented in Section 4.3. However, since the
discrete phase has proved troublesome to simulate, it has been decided to model the air as a single
centrally located bubble with radius b. This central bubble will be implemented in this Chapter,
by taking into account that it may also be a function of time.
6.1 Moving Grid
The present problem involves a drying droplet, thus the outer radius of the droplet, R, is a function
of drying time. Furthermore, the droplet may contain a single centrally located bubble with radius
b, which may also vary with time. The models for the diﬀerent phases in the droplet can in this
case be solved numerically with FD and by utilizing one of the following methods for dealing with
moving boundaries:
• Fixed grid: The grid used in FD is ﬁxed, such that the spatial location of each grid point
is ﬁxed. The advantages of this method is that one does not need to deal with the problems
arising from the grid being time dependent. On the contrary, the boundaries of the system
may lie in between grid points, and thus one needs to interpolate the solution close to the
boundary, or make sure that the employed time steps always causes the boundary to be on a
grid point. Secondly, as time goes, the number of grid points in the droplet would decrease
and thereby also the accuracy of the solution.
• Moving grid: With this method the amount of grid points are ﬁxed, but the spatial location
of each varies with time. The advantages of this method is that the boundaries are always
placed at a grid point, which insures that the numerical diﬀusion is kept at a minimum.
However, the disadvantages is that one has to incorporate the movement of each grid point
as a function of time.
Even though it may be troublesome to include the time dependency of the grid, the method of a
moving grid has been used in this thesis.
In order to make the simulation of the moving grid easier, a new dimensionless space variable is
introduced:
z(t, r) =
r −b(t)
R(t) −b(t)
(6.1.1)
where R(t) is the outer radius of the droplet, and b(t) is the radius of a possible inner bubble, as
seen in Figure 6.1. From hereon it is implied that R and b may be functions of time. The new space

The solution is solved in MATLAB® using the function Hands_cont_timedep_nobubble which
is solved in the script call_Hands_cont_timdep using an ODE45-solver. The code for both the
function and the script are given in Appendix B.3, and the results of interest are plotted in Figure
6.2.
Figure 6.2 (a) depicts the overall mass fraction of B within the droplet as a function of time, and (b)
shows the total mass of B as a function of time. Since the radius of the droplet decreases with time,
as given by (6.2.7), and no B but only A evaporates from the droplet, it would be assumed that
total mass of B would stay constant but the overall mass fraction of B would increase. However,
Figure 6.2 (a) depicts a constant overall mass fraction of B in time, and (b) shows a decreasing total
mass of B. This indicates that both A and B evaporates in the mass ratio 9:1, causing the overall
mass fraction of B to stay constant with time.
This result is explained by a faulty BC at z = 1. The above simulation has used a BC 2 that
states that the total ﬂux at r = R must be 0. However, this is incorrect since the moving droplet
surface imposes a ﬂux on the remaining continuous phase. Thus, the second BC is corrected to the
following:
BC 2:
nr,n+1(t) = ρc
n+1ωc

Chapter 7
Simulation of Droplet With Heat Balance
At this point the complete model for the continuous phase has been implemented, under the as-
sumption that no discrete phase is present or forms, ε = 0. However, the expressions for R(t) and
b(t) are simple linear expression without any physical grounding, thus these have to be reﬁned. In
order to ﬁnd the correct relation between R(t) and b(t) a series of balances for the system are set
up, and the Newton-Raphson method is used for solving them.
At ﬁrst, the Newton-Raphson method for multiple equations will be presented, and secondly the
relevant balances of the system will be presented for two cases; constant mass rate of evaporation
and time dependent mass rate of evaporation. In the last part of this chapter, a full simulation of
a droplet will be presented.
7.1 Newton-Raphson Method
The Newton-Raphson method is a widely used method for locating roots of one or a set of functions,
f1(x), f2(x), . . . , fn(x), where x is a vector containing all the variables. The method is derived
from a ﬁrst order Taylor approximation, which for a one-variable function is as follows:
f(xi+1) = f(xi) + f′(xi)(xi+1 −xi)
(7.1.1)
Assuming that xi, f(xi), and f′(xi) are all known, the x value at which f(xi+1) = 0 can be
approximated as:
xi+1 = xi + f(xi+1) −f(xi)
f′(xi)
= xi + 0 −f(xi)
f′(xi)
= xi −f(xi)
f′(xi)
(7.1.2)
xi+1 is not the exact location of the root but it is most likely closer than xi. Thus, by using xi+1 as
the next initial guess one obtains an x-coordinate that is even closer to the reel root. The method
is graphically displayed in Figure 7.1. For a set of n equations, with a maximum of n variables, the
above equation becomes:
xi+1 = xi −f(xi) J−1
(7.1.3)
where f(xi) is a vector of the functions evaluated at xi, and J is the Jacobian matrix, consisting
of the partial derivatives:
J =


∂f1
∂x1
∂f1
∂x2
. . .
∂f1
∂xn
∂f2
∂x1
∂f2
∂x2
. . .
∂f2
∂xn
...
...
...
∂fn
∂x1
∂fn
∂x2
. . .
∂fn
∂xn


(7.1.4)
The advantages of this method is that it is a fast method with quadratic converges if the initial
guess, xi, is a good approximation. On the contrary, if the initial guess is poor the method may
67
==PAGE:82==
7.2 Mass, Energy and Pressure Balance
Figure 7.1: Graphical depiction of the Newton-Raphson
method. Reprinted from Chapra [19].
never converge, since the method is an open bracket method, which means that xi can take on any
calculated value, since no upper or lower limit has been set. Furthermore, if a point with a zero
gradient is reached, the method collapses[19].
The Newton-Raphson method is incorporated in the function newtonm, as given in Appendix B.5,
and is developed by Urroz[23]. Urroz set the tolerance at 1 · 10−10, but when solving the problems
put forth in this thesis, the tolerance had to be raised to 1 · 10−3, since otherwise no solution was
found. This is of course a problem if the solution is not precise enough, and it is a point of interest
for future work.
7.2 Mass, Energy and Pressure Balance
Two cases of the drying droplet have been investigated:
• Constant drying rate: where it is assumed that the total mass rate of evaporation of solvent
and the droplet temperature are constant.
• Decreasing drying rate: where it is assumed that the total mass rate of evaporation of solvent
is a function of the mass fraction of solvent on the outer surface of the droplet, and the droplet
temperature is no longer constant.
Throughout the simulation presented in this chapter the values used, unless anything else is stated,
are those listed in Table 5.1, 5.2, and 7.1.
Table 7.1: Values used for simulation of droplet when using mvap.

7.2 Mass, Energy and Pressure Balance
where k and ∆Hvap are given at 100℃, and Cp is given at 90℃. The Nusselt number is given by
the following relation for forced convection around a sphere[13]:
Nu = 2 + 0.60 Re1/2Pr1/3
(7.2.1)
where Re is the Reynolds number, and Pr is Prandtls number. However, in this simulation it is
assumed that the surrounding gas is stagnant, hence Re is 0, and thus Nu = 2.
7.2.1
Constant Mass Rate of Evaporation
The ﬁrst case to be simulated is when it is assumed that the total mass rate of evaporation and the
droplet temperature are constant, as is the case in the constant drying rate period, see Figure 2.3.
7.2.1.1

mass balance, mass balance of B, and a pressure balance.

7.2.2
Time Dependent Mass Rate of Evaporation
The second case of interest when taking into account the temperature and total mass rate of
evaporation, is the case where the total mass rate of evaporation is decreasing, due to formation
of a diﬀusion barrier in the form of a beginning droplet shell. This is simulated by assuming that
the mass rate of evaporation can be explained merely as a function of the mass fraction of A at the
surface:
mvap(t) = mvap(t0) −
ωc
A,n+1(t0) −ωc
A,n+1(t)
ωc
A,n+1(t0)
mvap(t0)
(7.2.20)
This is a very approximative expression, and a more realistic expression should be derived. When
the mass rate of evaporation decreases, but the temperature of the gas stays the same, as it is
assumed here, the temperature of the droplet must increase, thus the temperature can no longer be
assumed constant.
7.2.2.1
Balances
The balances put forth in the previous section, (7.2.3), (7.2.6), and (7.2.14), are also valid for this
case, the only diﬀerence being that now the temperature is also a function of time, thus a fourth
balance for the energy of the system must be derived.

7.3 Simulation of Droplet

and constant mass rate of evaporation, as in Section 7.2.1, but after some time the mass rate of
evaporation starts to decrease due to initial formation of a shell, which causes a diﬀusion barrier
towards the droplet surface, as in Section 7.2.2.

However, the derived model is not fully developed, i.e. no discrete phase is present or forms, and
thus nu shell consisting of crystallized B is formed. Nonetheless, it is assumed that a shell begins
to form when the mass fraction of B at the outer radius, R, is 0.65, as also used by Handscomb[1].
The resulting graphs are shown in Figure 7.8 and 7.9, where the vertical gray stippled line indicates
the shift from a constant to a time dependent mass rate of evaporation.
Figure 7.8 (a) is a plot of the inner and outer radius of the droplet as a function of time, and as is
seen the outer radius decreases from 1 to approximately 0.42 in 120 s, from whereon it is constant.
The reason why a constant radius is obtained is seen in Figure 7.9 (a) where the overall mass fraction
of B is plotted as a function of time. At approximately 120 s the overall mass fraction of B is 1,
and thus no further evaporation occurs.
In Figure 7.8 (a) the inner radius is also plotted as function of time. In the ﬁrst region, the radius
is constant, while when changing from a constant to a time dependent mass rate, the inner droplet
radius increases to approximately 0.105 mm, as also seen in Figure 7.5 (b). The explanation of this is
seen in Figure 7.8 (b) where the droplet temperature is shown as a function of time. When shifting
from constant to time dependent mass rate of evaporation, the temperature increases steeply to
near gas temperature, and a increase in droplet temperature, also causes an increase in pressure in
the air bubble, and thus a small ballooning of the droplet is observed.
As mentioned in the previous section, the sudden increase in droplet temperature seems unrealistic,
and is explained by faulty constant values of Cp, ∆Hvap, and k. Furthermore, Figure 7.9 (b) depicts
the total mass of B as a function of time, and from this it is seen that when shifting from the
constant to the time dependent mass rate of evaporation, the mass of B increases with 1.17%. The
increase in mass of B is most likely due to a faulty shift in models, and is a point of improvement.
76
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 8
Discussion and Future Work
At this point, a model for a drying droplet with entrapped air inside, has been developed. However,
the model is a somewhat simpliﬁed model in which several assumptions are used, and the validity
of these assumptions will be discussed in this chapter. Furthermore, a sensitivity analysis will be
conducted of the simulated model, in which the amount of spatial and time nodes used will be
accessed. Lastly, a section describing the future work needed to be done on the model will be
presented.
8.1 Sensitivity analysis
A sensitivity analysis is conducted, in which it is examined what eﬀect the number of spatial nodes
in the FD solutions have, as well as the eﬀect of time steps used in the ODE45-solver. For this
analysis, the system simulating a droplet with a centrally located bubble and moving inner and
outer boundary, as described in Section 6.2.3, has been chosen, since this is the model used in the
ﬁnal simulation of the droplet although with simple linear expressions of the inner and outer radius.
The mass of B should be the same at all times, since no B is to leave the droplet, and thus the
mass of B is chosen as the measure of precision of the model. The error in mass of B is calculated
as follows:
Em = mB(100 s) −mB(0 s)
mB(0 s)
· 100%
(8.1.1)
where the mass of B at a given time, mB, is calculated using (5.2.34). In Table 8.1 the results of
the sensitivity analysis are given, where the error in mass has been determined when using varying
amounts of spatial and time steps to solve the system. Furthermore, the time it took to simulate
the system at the given numbers of steps is also listed.
When keeping the amount of spatial steps constant but varying the amount of time steps, it is seen
that the error in mass do not change, and neither does the simulation time signiﬁcantly. Thus it is
concluded that time steps above 50 do not inﬂuence the precision of the solution, and hence should
Table 8.1: Simulation time and error in mass as a function of spatial and time steps

-0.0448

3.02
-0.0448

-0.0448

14.84
-0.0211
100
100
15.11
-0.0211
100

15.35
-0.0211
200

-0.0151

==PAGE:92==
8.2 Discussion
be kept at 50 to reduce simulation time. The reason why the number of time steps is insigniﬁcant
could be that when using the ODE45-solver it simulates the system at times determined within the
solver, and merely matches the solution found at the internal point with the times speciﬁed by the
user to the solver.
On the contrary, when the amount of spatial steps is doubled, the simulation time is approximately
5 doubled. Furthermore, when going from 50 to 100 spatial steps the error in mass is halved, and
when going from 100 to 200 steps the error in mass is decreased with 28%. In conclusion, the
amount of spatial steps is of great signiﬁcance to the precision of the results.
Since, when going from 100 to 200 spatial steps, the error in mass only decreases with 28%, corre-
sponding to 53% when going from 50 to 100 spatial steps, and the error in mass is as low as 0.02%
with 100 spatial steps, the simulations in this thesis have been simulated using 100 spatial steps.
The number of time steps do no inﬂuence the precision of the model, but merely the resulting plots,
the number of time steps used has also been set to 100.
8.2 Discussion
In this section a discussion of the assumptions made and of the model in general will be given.
8.2.1
Assumptions
Throughout the derivation and implementation of the model, several assumptions have been made.
The validity of these will be discussed here.
First of all, throughout the entire derivation and implementation it has been assumed that spherical
symmetry exists within the droplet. This may be true if the droplet do not move, but in a spray
dryer where the droplets are sprayed out at the top and falls to the bottom of the chamber, the
assumption of a fully uniform spherical droplet seems unrealistic. The velocity of the droplet will
cause the droplet to deform, i.e. to a bean like shape, and thus the evaporation of the droplet is no
longer uniform. Even if the overall shape of the droplet remains spherical, spherical symmetry is no
longer obtained, since the "front" of the droplet will experience increased mass and heat transfer
compared to the rest of the droplet. However, modeling a droplet that do not experience spherical
symmetry is cumbersome, and thus the assumption of a spherically symmetric droplet is often made.
As already mentioned a droplet in a spray dryer is formed at the top of the chamber and falls down
to the bottom of the chamber, hence the droplet moves. Furthermore, the gas in the chamber is also
not stagnant, since it may move in a co- or counter-current ﬂow pattern with the spray in which
the temperature of the gas decreases due to heating and evaporation of the droplets. However, in
this thesis it is assumed that the gas and droplet is stagnant and that the temperature of the gas
is constant. These assumptions are not valid when modeling a drying droplet inside a spray dryer,
and thus part of the future work is to implement the varying gas temperature, and velocity of the
droplet and gas in the heat balance.
The way entrapped air is introduced to the model is as a single centrally located bubble. Initially
the idea was to implement air bubbles as a discrete phase by a model similar to the one derived in
Section 5.3. However, the implementation and simulation of the discrete phase proved troublesome,
and thus entrapped air was introduced as a single centrally located bubble instead. Initially the
entrapped air may enter the droplet in numerous small bubbles, but after some time the assumption
of one centrally located air bubble may be valid, since the smaller air bubbles gather to one larger
78
==PAGE:93==
8.3 Future Work
bubble in order to minimize surface tension. However, the assumption that the air bubble is centrally
located is merely an assumption that makes the model and simulation easier.
8.2.2
Model
The model developed in Chapter 4 has been solved by using the FD method on the spatial variable,
resulting in an ordinary diﬀerential equation in time which was solved using the ODE45-solver
in MATLAB®. The FD method is an approximation method where the spatial derivatives are
approximated by utilizing Taylor expansion, thus introducing truncation errors, but as discovered
in the previous section, the overall error in mass that was observed with both 100 spatial and time
steps were merely 0.0211 %, which is assumed to be satisfactory.
However, the implemented ﬁnal model, Section 7.3, is very sensitive towards the value of the con-
stants used in the model, i.e. the model is not robust, which is of course a problem. One explanation
for the sensitivity of the model is that the implementation of the model is faulty or unnecessarily
complicated, especially when using the Newton-Raphson method. The inner and outer radius of
the droplet are determined at each internal time step in the ODE45-solver before solving the ordi-
nary diﬀerential equation for the continuous phase in time. This could probably be optimized by
implementing the four balances used to ﬁnd the inner and outer radius, directly into the function
solved by the ODE45-solver, and is thus a point for future work.
In the ﬁnal simulation of the droplet, Section 7.3, a small increase in b was observed. This phenomena
is called ballooning, since it causes the droplet to expand even though evaporation of solvent occurs.
However, the increase in b was only approximately 5%, even though the diﬀerence between the
droplet temperature and gas temperature was 60℃. Thus, the conclusion from that simulation
must be that the eﬀect of ballooning is neglectable under the simulated conditions. However, with
a more realistic heat balance and more realistic values of the constants used in the heat balance,
the conclusion may be diﬀerent.
8.3 Future Work
In this thesis a model for the drying of a droplet with entrapped air inside has been developed with
the model by Handscomb as the base. However, several things are not complete and needs further
work. The main points for future work will be presented here.
First of all, the model for the discrete phase was solved when the diﬀusion constant of the discrete
phase in the continuous phase was assumed to be constant. However, with a size dependent diﬀusion
coeﬃcient, an unrealistic solution was obtained. This was of course unsatisfying, and thus further
work have to be put into solving the discrete phase in a satisfying manner. Furthermore, when a
satisfactory simulation of the discrete phase has been obtained, simulation of the continuous and
the discrete phase should be coupled.
In Chapter 7 a set of equations relating the inner and outer radius as solvent evaporates from the
sphere was put forth. Two cases were examined; constant drying rate and time dependent drying
rate. In the ﬁrst case it was assumed that the temperature was constant, and in the second the mass
rate of evaporation decreases, and with less evaporation, more energy goes to heating the droplet,
hence the temperature increases. The expression used for the mass rate of evaporation was a simple
linear function of the mass fraction of solvent at the surface of the droplet, and thus a point of
future work is to obtain a more realistic expression for the mass rate of evaporation.
79
==PAGE:94==
8.3 Future Work
Furthermore, the values for Cp, k, and ∆Hvap used in the energy balance were constant values
for water at a given temperature. On the way to obtain a more realistic model, temperature and
composition dependent functions for the three constants have to be found. The sensitivity analysis
performed has only focused on the spatial nodes and time steps, however, it should be extended to
cover the sensitivity of a change in one or more of the constants used. The conclusion from this
extended sensitivity analysis could be used to determine the importance of a precise determination
of the constants.
It should be noted that all results presented in this thesis are only based on simulation, and hence
no experiments have been made in order to verify the model. Therefore, part of the future work is
also to perform experiments in order to verify the drying properties as determined by the model
Lastly, the model presented in this thesis only covers drying of the droplet prior to rigid shell
formation. Thus in order to have a full drying model, one needs to develop models for the drying
droplet during and after shell formation.
80
==PAGE:95==
Chapter 9
Conclusion
The subject of this thesis is modeling of a single droplet drying when air is entrapped inside. The
overall goal has been to understand and derive an already existing model for a single droplet drying
with no air inside, and subsequently expand it to model entrapped air as well.
The ﬁrst step towards the goal, was to conduct a literature review in order to obtain the necessary
knowledge of how the spray drying technique works, as well as examine the already known models
for drying droplets. It was found that four main approaches are used when modeling single drying
droplets, the four being a characteristic drying curve, reaction engineering approach, conservation
equations, and lastly population balance equation. The approach used in this report is conservation
equations and a population balance equation for the discrete phase.
The model by Handscomb[1] was chosen as the base model on which further work was done, since
it is able to model a droplet consisting of both a continuous and a discrete phase, in which the
continuous phase contains solvent and solute and the discrete phase can either be a insoluble material
or crystallized solute. The model divides droplet drying into two main regimes; prior to and after
formation of a rigid shell, of which only the ﬁrst was derived in this thesis. Furthermore, due to
limited time, the complete heat balance was not derived, and instead a simpliﬁed expression for the
total mass rate of evaporation was adopted.
The next step towards the goal was to implement and solve the models using MATLAB®, in which
the ﬁnite diﬀerence method was used for the spatial variable. The implementation was done in
several steps with increasing complexity, with the ﬁnal one being simulation of the continuous
phase with a centrally located air bubble inside, and where both the inner and outer radius of the
droplet was simple functions of time. However, implementation and simulation of the discrete phase
proved troublesome, and was therefore not completed.
In the ﬁnal part of this thesis, balances describing the inner and outer radius of the droplet when
also taking into account the mass rate of evaporation and droplet temperature, was put forth. These
were used for simulation of two diﬀerent cases for the mass rate of evaporation; constant mass rate
of evaporation and decreasing mass rate of evaporation and thus increase in droplet temperature,
as would be expected when a shell begins to form. The two cases were combined and the ballooning
eﬀect, caused by expansion of the internal air bubble, was observed. However, the constants used
in the heat balance was assumed to be constant values throughout the entire drying, and thus not
functions of temperature or droplet composition, and may therefore give a faulty simulation.
In conclusion, a model describing drying of a single droplet containing a centrally located air bubble
has been derived, implemented and simulated, and ballooning of the droplet was observed. The
model describing the discrete phase of the droplet, hence the crystallized solute, has been derived,
put not implemented. Furthermore, several simplifying assumptions have been made. Nonetheless,
the model put forth in this thesis is a starting point for a complete model describing drying of
droplets with entrapped air.

A.1 Background
A function, γα, will be deﬁned such that it has value of unity in the α-phase, but is zero in all other
phases, which collectively will be referred to as the β-phase. A building block for γα is the unit step
function, H, given in one dimension as follows:

A.2 Averaging Deﬁnitions
In order to apply the volume averaging technique to a continuous ﬂow problem, an averaging volume
have to be determined. The size and shape of the averaging volume has to be independent of time
and space, thus the characteristic length of the averaging volume has to be much greater than
the pore diameters of the material, but much less than the characteristic length of the medium.
Furthermore, as speciﬁed in Subsection 4.1.1, the size of the averaging volume has to be such that
the volume average of a quantity at a given point is almost independent of position when one moves
in the order of magnitude of the characteristic length of the averaging volume.
For averaging purposes it is convenient to introduce a local coordinate system, ξ1, ξ2 and ξ3, within
the averaging volume, V i. The coordinates axis of the ξ-system are parallel to x1, x2, and x3, but
the origin is at point x, as depicted in Figure A.4.
Figure A.4: Depiction of the local coordinate sys-
tem ξ within the averaging volume. Reprinted from
Gray et al. [18].

(A.3.22)
= ∂B
(α)
∂t
−1
V
Z
Sw
(Bw · ˆn) dA
(A.3.23)
where it has been used that when taking the volume integral of δ, it is equal to taking the surface
integral at the surface where δ is not 0. Furthermore, the multiplication rule of a dot product4 have
been used.
4a(b · c) = [ab · c], where a may either be a scalar, vector or tensor function, while b and c are vector functions.

B.1.2
⟨vr⟩(c) = kr
In subsection 5.2.2 a simpliﬁed version of the continuous phase was presented, in which it was
assumed that ⟨vr⟩(c) = kr. The MATLAB® function in which the solution was implemented is
Hands_cont_kr, which is presented below.
1
function dw=Hands_cont_kr(t,w)
2
3
global D k0 tau nr dr
4
5
% Boundary conditions
6
dw(1,1)
= 6*D/dr^2*(w(2,1)−w(1,1))−3*k0*(1−exp(−t/tau))*w(1,1);
7
dw(nr+1,1) = 2*D/dr^2*(w(nr,1)−w(nr+1,1))...
8
−k0*(1−exp(−t/tau))*w(nr+1,1)*(k0*(1−exp(−t/tau))*nr^2*dr^2/D+1−2*nr);
9
10
% Internal equations
11
for i=2:nr
12
dw(i,1) = D/((i−1)*dr^2)*(i*w(i+1,1)−2*(i−1)*w(i,1)+(i−2)*w(i−1,1))...
13
−k0*(1−exp(−t/tau))/2*((i−1)*w(i+1,1)+6*w(i,1)−(i−1)*w(i−1,1));
14
end
15
end

B.2.1
Constant D(d)
When it is assumed that the diﬀusion constant in the discrete phase, D(d), is constant, the function
used to solve the model as presented in Section 5.3.1, is Hands_disc_constD:
1
function dm=mfunc(t,m)

B.2.2
Size-Dependent Diﬀusion Constant
In Section 5.3.2 the model for the discrete phase when having a size-dependent diﬀusion constant
was put forth, and this was implemented into the function Hands_disc_varD:
1
function dm = Hands_disc_varD(t,m)
2
3
global L_min N0 G kb Td mu nr dr
4
5
% Input values
6
m0 = m(0*(nr+1)+1:1*(nr+1),1);
7
m1 = m(1*(nr+1)+1:2*(nr+1),1);
8
m2 = m(2*(nr+1)+1:3*(nr+1),1);
9
m3 = m(3*(nr+1)+1:4*(nr+1),1);
10
11
% Calculated values
12
mm1 = m0.^2./m1;
13
14
% Initializing
15
dm0 = zeros(nr+1,1);
16
dm1 = zeros(nr+1,1);
17
dm2 = zeros(nr+1,1);
18
dm3 = zeros(nr+1,1);
19
20
% at r = 0
21
dm0(1,1)=N0
+2*kb*Td/(pi*mu*dr^2)*(mm1(2,1)−mm1(1,1));
22
dm1(1,1)=L_min^1*N0 + 1*G*m0(1,1)+2*kb*Td/(pi*mu*dr^2)*(m0(2,1)−m0(1,1));
23
dm2(1,1)=L_min^2*N0 + 2*G*m1(1,1)+2*kb*Td/(pi*mu*dr^2)*(m1(2,1)−m1(1,1));
24
dm3(1,1)=L_min^3*N0 + 3*G*m2(1,1)+2*kb*Td/(pi*mu*dr^2)*(m2(2,1)−m2(1,1));
25
26
% for r = R
27
dm0(nr+1,1) = N0...
28
+ 2*kb*Td/(3*pi*mu*dr^2)*(mm1(nr,1)−mm1(nr+1,1));
29
dm1(nr+1,1) = L_min^1*N0 + 1*G*m0(nr+1,1)...
30
+ 2*kb*Td/(3*pi*mu*dr^2)*(m0(nr,1)−m0(nr+1,1));
31
dm2(nr+1,1) = L_min^2*N0 + 2*G*m1(nr+1,1)...
32
+ 2*kb*Td/(3*pi*mu*dr^2)*(m1(nr,1)−m1(nr+1,1));
33
dm3(nr+1,1) = L_min^3*N0 + 3*G*m2(nr+1,1)...
34
+ 2*kb*Td/(3*pi*mu*dr^2)*(m2(nr,1)−m2(nr+1,1));
35
36

37
for i = 2:nr
38
dm0(i,1) = N0...
39
+ kb*Td/(3*pi*mu*(i−1)*dr^2)*...

(i*mm1(i+1,1)−2*(i−1)*mm1(i,1)−(i−2)*mm1(i−1,1));
41

42
+ kb*Td/(3*pi*mu*(i−1)*dr^2)*...
43
(i*m0(i+1,1)−2*(i−1)*m0(i,1)−(i−2)*m0(i−1,1));

B.3.1
Without A Centrally Located Air Bubble
In Subsection 6.2.1 a simpliﬁed version of the continuous phase where presented, in which it was as-
sumed that no centrally located bubble was present. The MATLAB® function in which the solution
was implemented is Hands_cont_timedep_nobubble, and Hands_cont_timedep_nobubble_nr,
which are presented below.
1
function dw=Hands_cont_timedep_nobubble(t,w)
2
3
global D L nr dz
4
[R, dRdt] = Rfunc(t);
5
6
% Boundary conditions
7
dw(1,1)
= 6*D/(dz^2*R^2)*(w(2,1)−w(1,1));
8
dw(nr+1,1) = 2*D/(dz^2*R^2)*(w(nr,1)−w(nr+1,1));
9
10
% Internal equations
11
for i=2:nr
12
dw(i,1) = D/(R^2*(i−1)*dz^2)*(i*w(i+1,1)−2*(i−1)*w(i,1)...
13
+(i−2)*w(i−1,1)−(i−1)/(2*(L+w(i,1)))*(w(i+1,1)−w(i−1,1))^2)...
14
+(i−1)/(2*R)*dRdt*(w(i+1,1)−w(i−1,1));
15
end
16
end
1
function dw=Hands_cont_timedep_nobubble_nr(t,w)
2
3
global D L nr dz
4
5
[R, dRdt] = Rfunc(t);
6
7
% Boundary conditions
8
dw(1,1)
= 6*D/(dz^2*R^2)*(w(2,1)−w(1,1));
9
dw(nr+1,1) = 2*D/(R^2*dz^2)*(w(nr,1)−w(nr+1,1))...
10
−(2*(nr+1))/R*(L+w(nr+1,1))/L*dRdt*w(nr+1,1)...
11
−1/D*dRdt^2*(w(nr+1,1)+3*w(nr+1,1)^2/L+2*w(nr+1,1)^3/L^2);
12
13
% Internal equations
14
for i=2:nr
15
dw(i,1) = D/(R^2*(i−1)*dz^2)*(i*w(i+1,1)−2*(i−1)*w(i,1)...
16
+(i−2)*w(i−1,1)−(i−1)/(2*(L+w(i,1)))*(w(i+1,1)−w(i−1,1))^2)...
17
+(i−1)/(2*R)*dRdt*(w(i+1,1)−w(i−1,1));
18
end
19
end

B.4.1
Constant Droplet Temperature
In Section 7.2.1, it is assumed that the droplet dries at a constant temperature. The functions used
to describe this situation is given in f3:
1
function f = f3(x,t)
2
3
global Rgas patm rho0 n y mvap rhoA L R0 b0 wB0 T0
4
b
= x(1);
5
R
= x(2);
6
wBt = x(3);
7
8
F1 = rhoA*L/(L+wBt)*4/3*pi*(R^3−b^3)−rho0*4/3*pi*(R0^3−b0^3) + mvap*t;
9
F2 = wBt*rhoA*L/(L+wBt)*(R^3−b^3)−wB0*rho0*(R0^3−b0^3);
10
F3 = n*Rgas*T0/(4/3*pi*b^3)−patm−2*y*(1/R+1/b);
11
f
= [F1;F2;F3];
12
end
